Foreword
I must admit that when I was first asked to act as an advisor to the publisher in the translation
and technical aspects of what follows, I was a bit skeptical. I assumed then, as I still do, that
the manual deals with a subjecl, the depth and sophistication of which can hardly be grasped
by the uniniliated through a book alone. When I first went to Japan, before I was the recipient
of so much kindness from my teachers and sponsors there, I hardly could havs guessed at
the complexity of Japanese carpentry technique and the sheer (almost bottomless) depth of
knowledge and tradition tied up with it.
Japanese carpenlry, as so many of the crafts of Japan, did not spring fully developed from
the mind of some gonius. Rather, Japanese carpontry and architecture are the products of
1500 yoars (in Japan alone, not to mention China, their origin) of cumulative knowledge, collected and passed on from leacher to disciple, ln my case, the transmission was directly to
me from my teacher, over,a five year period, working 70-BO hours per week, I might add this
was thought minimal, but enough to get tho basics down. Fifteen years is considered necessary for becomlng a temple carpenter, lhe equivalent of 30 years al 40 hours a week.
Many many more books would be nece$sary to deal with the basics, I refer to manuals on
tool use, layout, architectural styles, not to mention teahouses or temples.
I strongly suggest that one gnter into this work with a kind of boldness and take the plunge,
but I also caution the reader that one cannot learn alone. One can learn something, and that
is good, but by tho use of this manual alone ono will not become a Japaneso carpenter,
,Also I should add that becoming good*reaily good-takes a lot of time, This manual is
analogous lo sheet music. To an accomplished musician who dogsn't havo to think about how
to play an instrument, sheet music is valuable, but owning the sheet music will not toach one
how to play. Con$tant practise, for years, of repetitive tasks alone develops a sense of square
and straight and leads to a fluency with tools, as with learning a foreign language, whlch ln
turn leads to a freedom to make things as one wants them,
All this is not io say that this manual is ol no use, There is much to use in thls book and much
one can bonow. I would hope that Westerners see what is useful and applicable to their needs

## S. 4

and then to use it. Just as the Japanese in th6 past borrowed extensively from China and the
west, and also have been able to forge dlstlnctive versions of their own, I would hope that North
Amerlcans would, in turn, be able to do the same. There ls great room for innovation, particularly where new technologies are now available to doal with architectural prootems- ' -: "--
The diagrams of joints will probably elicit the greatest response. However, it is interesting to
note that th€ design of the jolnts is really quite simple and that, of all the things in the book,
layout is considerably more dlfficult.
This work is physical. The layout, also, is physical, western geometry ls the product (as far
as I know) of an intellectual process, a process for which I have grbat respect, This Japanese
layout, however, is experiontial*it ls "hands on"-and dears with a carpenter,s ability to visualize on a three-dimensionar scale. Though "recipes" for layout are included, , wourd urge the
reader to try to understand the whyon a perceptuallevel.
ln this book, I want to draw attention to the importance of the center line ( { ), Att loints must
be drawn and laid out, from the center line outwards in both directions. Otherwise oonfusion
will almost certainly result ln pieces whose length is off. All distances and spans are made
from center line to center line, Then, where joints are used, the carp€nter works back from the
center line, if necessary, ln the same way, spans broken into segments are broken on the conter line. sgch a system is necessary, especially when constructing a building, as there simply
are too many joints to keep track of . So always go from the center line, even if it seems an extra step"
One final note. Those joints which arg structurat must be very tight, They alone hold up the
roof , and the roof is both heavy and dangerous, A loose joint cannot be trusted, especially if il
serves a critical structural purpose,
This book, it seems to me, can be either an lnteresting excurslon or it can be the starl of a
lifetime study- Either way, I think, it has great value, and I slncerely hope it is used,
April 29, 1gB3
Lon Brackett
Nevada City, Ca,

The text ol Japanese Woodworking has been clarified and expanded to offer encouragement to Western woodworkers and would-be woodworkers not familiar with the versatility and
extent of joinery possible through skilled handwork. Basic woodworking processes and setups have been stated in a straightforward manner that can be applied to woodworking in any
style, There are fine points of technique that are developed only with hands-on experience,
but each of these has its feet, hands, heart and head rooled in the Basics The attention to
detail and the refinement o1 skills in the initial chores of simple sharpening and lool care are
crucial to the work reaching its peak in ihe more complicated forms of joinery,
Another maior area ol this work that needs comment is the joinery itself' A clear mental
picture ot tho layout, geometry and space configurations involved in oach joint must be built
up from the Simpler to the more complex configurations. Start work with the most
elementary joinery, Once skill in creating these becomes accurate and predictable, it can be
applied to the compound joints and practiced until it becomes a reflex, When building
loinery it is very helpful to visualize and iabricate it from the inside out, instead of removing
the oasy, obvious parts first from the outside. This approach helps to determine the proper
sequence for mortising and sawing, ln this way error and inefJiciency resulting from reworking
the layout lines can be greatly minimized. The work can thus proceed in a speedier and more
organized fashion, 
l
The opportunity to aontribute to an effort such as this book has drawn upon my years of
experience as a proiessional Japanese-stylo woodworker, tool demonstrator and teacher
of Japanese tools and joinery processes.
The diversity and originality of Japanese woodworking provide insight into the complexity
of relationships with Nature which reilect and shape the character, value and practices of
Japanese culture, As a fellow Keniuckian, Wendell Beny, has noted, the universal law of Song
is that one must enter singing, lt is hoped that even if you cannot sing the lead yourself with
certainty, with the help of this book you can at least hum along'
Jay van Arsdale
The Pull of the Saw
South Oakland, Ca,
Author's Preface to
,Iapane$e Joinery
Though there is greater utilization of steel and reinforced concrele in modern structures,
wood construction remains among tho most imporlant, maintaining its long tradition in
Japanese architecture. Ftegretfully however, with the passing of time, the splendid tradi.
tlon of Japanese wood conslructlon has become stagnanl and is losing its driving force.
Though it is essential for architects and builders to understand traditional Japanese
wood construction, texts on the subject are woelully llmited. This could be due to the difficullie$ of doscribing and lllustratlng the actual woodwork, but there are other limiting tactors as well. ln many cases, a woed member serves a dual purpose as a structural member
and as a flnished decorative piece. Also, dlflerent methods are used in each school, such
as the Kennin Temple sect, shintennoji sect, qnd Kyoto sect. Moreover, differenl mothods
are stressed even within the same school by such master carpenters as Hiruuchi, Kira,
Kiuchi, and rsuru. Then, there are alteratlons in detailing methods brought about by
changes in the style of periods such as the Asuka [ca 700 A.D. - 900 A,D.], Momoyama [ca,
1550 A.D.-1600 A.D.l,'and Edo [ca. t600 A,D.*1860 A.D,] periods. There are further dif.
ferences in technique in styles such as lhe Karaya [chinese slyle), Tenjikuyo Ilndian styleJ,
wayo [Japanese style], sholnzukuri [sludy room styte], sukiyazukuri llrae stytel, and the
Chashilsu [toa room styleJ. Lastly, there are regional differences on such things as uchinori
[inside face], talami [straw mat], and klwari [proportion].
It is not an easy task to incorporate all these conditions. This book was wriilen using
my own practical experience combined with informatjon from available resources, and
from documents on various methods ol wood construction compiled during the time I was
teaching at the Occupational Training Center.

## S. 6

At present, there are numerous publlcatlons on new details. However, as in the proverb, "onko chishin" (not to throw the baby out wlth the bath water), it ls tirst necessary lo
revlew the past developmenl and progress of wood construction before creating new
methods.
I write this book in the hope that it will be of use to lhe reader, not only to architects
and to carpenters, but also to students, and the lay person"
I widh lo express my gratitude to Professor Tamaoki, Toyojiro, Professor ol engineerlng, for his guidance, and to the edlting departrnent of Rikogakusha for its lnvaluable
assistanc6.

## S. 7

CHAPTER I
THE WORKPLACE
The Work Area
Sufficient space is all that is needed for a work area. No spscial set-up is required. However,
a flat site free from wind is preferable. lt is also important that work be done out of direct
sunlight. Exposure to sunlight may cause wooden plane bodies to warp and twist, and
sharpening stones will dry out and crack. When out ol doors, work in the shado of a tree or
other building. lndoors, the work area should be well lit, but still, care should be taken to
prevent direct light lrom the sun striking work sr.r'rfaces.
The work area should be clean and orderly, to avoid hazards. The floor of the work area
should be coverqd with carpeting ot tatamis. This makes standing more comfortable and
protects sensitive tocl blades from damage sliould they drop to the ground.
Glothlng
Clothing should be comfortable to work in and made of a material that is easily washed and
cleaned. lt should allow for lree movement of lhe arms and legs, since Japanese carpentry
involves much physical movement, squatting and bending over. Balance and conlrol ol
bodily motion are all important, so restrictive clothing would not be comfortable.
I

2 The Workplace
The llUork Table
Having a work table makes the work easier and less tiring' and is a must when working with
Smallpieces.TheworksurfaceshouldbeslightlyhigherthanWaistheight.allowinglor
movementottheshoulderswithoutrestriction.Theworktableisgenerallyusedlorlaylng
out or marking pieces, or for gluing' Sawing and planing are done either on saw horses or
on the planing bench. Although not used traditionally' a vice may be attached to a corner of
the table, though this is not necessary when working on framing members such as posts
and beams"
The Planlng Bench
TheheightofaplaningbenchShouldbedeterminedbythedimensionsofthoWorkman's
body.Thetopoftheplaningsurfaceshouldgrazetheknucklesoftheworkman,shand
when he is standing erect. Most ol the etfort in planing is made with the leg and stomach
muscles,withtheshouldersbeingusediorthefollow-through,soaworksurfaceatabout
hip height is preferable, When setting up the planing bench' one should adjust it to a height
which leels natural and comfortable. The wood for the bench top should be free of warp or
twists and should be absolutely flat to allow even pressure throughout the stroke of the
0i".". ,n" wgrk surface can sit on saw horses and should be well braced throughout its
iength, so thai it does not bow or bend when pressure is applied. For stability, the legs o{ an
outdoor planing bench may be set into the ground (see Figure 1'1)'

CHAPTER 2
TOOLS AND THEIR GARE
Artisans should keep in mind that thelr tools are their livelihood, Tools should always be
used and maintain€d with the greatest care. When professional-quality cuttinq tools are pur
chased, they are rarely ready to use immediately, Blade sharpening or other adjustments
may need to be done to put them in working order. Once sharpened, blades of edged tools
should be wiped with oil after each use. ln Japan, camellia oil i$ used. Walnut oil is also
good because it will not becomo Sticky or tacky, while vegotable oils become gooey and so
should be avoided. Petroleum-based oils should not be,used, since they will stain wood.
Only a light film of oil is necessary, and any residue or dirty oil should be wiped off.
Woodworkers and carpenters use many different kinds oi tools,and there are many variations among them, depending on the lype of construction boing done. However, the car
pente/s square (used to make measurements in addition to being a square), saw, plane,
ahisel, hammer, awl, and whetstones are a carpenter's basic working tools

## S. 10

6 Tools and Thelr Care
Figure 2.1. Carpenter's square,
The Carpenter's Square
Traditionally, the carpenter'S square was made of copper, However, stainless steel is used
today. ln either case, the material of which it is made should be flexible. The cross-section
of the square is shown in Figure 2,2a. Figure 2.2b shows how the square is easily pressed
down onto the objoct being measured by depressing the middle part with the thumb. Both
ends of the cr|ss-section are tapered so that the square doesn't smear ink when using it
with a pen or sumisalhi. Figure 2.3 shows the relationship between the scales on the front
and back side. The scales on the front, calibrated in sun (about 1 inch) and bu (about /e
inCh), are used lor linear measurement. The scales on the back can be used to determine
the length of the side of the square of the largest beam which can be made from a log, or the
circumference of the log itself.

I Tools and Their Gare
The types of eaws
unlike western saws, which are rated by the number ol teeth per inch' Japanese saws are
graded according to length, with the number ol te6th, especially in crosscut saws, being
roughly constant' Thus' a smaller, shorter saw has finer teeth than a longer saw.
Thelollowingarethedifferenttypesofsawsandtheirfunctions:
Tatebiki-Rip saw, Used for cutting with the grain'
Yokobiki-Crosscut saw, Used lor cutting across the grain'
Ryoba - Dauble-edged saw. This saw has both ripping and crosscutting sides For tinishing work, a smaller double-edged saw is used'
Dozuki - Back saw. Used to cut joints requiring absolutely flat surfaces' lt is used for
very {ine linish work, since the saw blade is very thin' the back of the blade is slipped into a
channel to strengthsn and reintorce it. There are both rip and crosscut back saws' crosscut
dozuki are used for cuts up to 45o from the perpendicular to the grain; rip dozukl are used
from this point on,
Keyhole saw. Used to cut out round and various other shapes The blade tapers to a
point.
Haoksaw. Used for cutting bamboo and hardwoods'
Sawyer's saw, Used for rough rip-cutting large pieces of lumber or trees'
Lumbor saw, Used lor rough crosscutting large pieces of lumber or trees'

Figure 2,11. Lumber Saw.
Both the sawyer's saw and the lumber saw have bigger, coarser teeth and a thicker blade
than regular saws because they are used for cutting green wood which tends to bind.
The above are some of the most commen saws. Aside from these, the marking gauge
(kebikil also severs wood. This tool, shown in Figure 2.12, comes in many sizes and is
preferable to the European lype because of its sharp blade which will not scratch or tear the
grain and which makes a very fine cut. Although it is not a saw, by increasing tho size of the
blade, it can be used to split thin boards by scoring them parallel to the grain. First, the blade
is sel at the gap required to cut the board to the desired size. Then, as shown in Figure
2,13a, b, the tool is placed al an edge of the board and pulled, scoring the board. Similar incisions are made on bolh sides of the board. By utilizing the edge of another object and
striking gently on the protruding portion of the board, the extra width is removed, as shown
in Figure 2,13c,
Aside from this use, the marking gauge is also used by carpenters and cabinetmakers to
dimension a piece of timber for planing. The line made with the gauge is diflerent from one
made with a pencil. Sinco the gauge line is a shallow cut, when the plane reaches the line,
the shaving peels off like paper, indicating the line has been reached. The line, however,
must be drawn with care because the tool tends to follow along the grain,

Figure 2.13 Using the marking gauge.
Japanese saws are pulled with a slight angle instead ol pushlng at a stoep anglo,
lf the saw is pulled in direction A of Figure 2.14, the wood is cut parallol to tho 0raln. Thig ls
called ripping.
Figure 2j4. Ripping and crosscutting.
When the saw is pulled in direction B, the wood is cut across (perpendicular to) the grain,
giving greater resistance to the cutting. This is called crosscutting.,Saw teeth are made elther ior ripping or for crosscutting, and aro shaped accordingly. The tips of both types of
teeth flare outward slightly so that the cuttlng surlace of the saw is wider than its body,
thereby preventing binding, (See Figures 2.15 and 2.'16,)

12 Toolr and Thelr Gare
The body. The body of a saw was traditionally made by softening and stretching a ball
I
ol steel; modorn saws are made lrom a sheet or ribbon of steel ground to the desired configuration, lf tho steel is too hard it will break or the teeth may chip. ll it is too solt it will not
hold an 6dge. The thickness of the body is tapered wilh the end teeth thin and gradually becoming thicker near the handle. This serves to Strongthen the saw and mako tho pulling
easier.
The teeth of a hardwood saw have a small, squat, and stable triangular profile. Saws for
cutting soft wood have a tooth profile consisting ol a longer, more sharply pointed triangle.
The shape ol the teeth of a rip saw used in sawing hardwood is shown in Figure 2.18a. The
top angle oJ each tooth is from 40o to 45o and back side is 90o to the body, Figure 218b
shows the shape of teeth on saws used for cutting softwood. The top angle is lrom 30o to
35o and the back, about 75o, Saws for crosscutting softwood have teeth wilh the top at an
angle of about 75o and the back angle at 90o, while the angle at the tip is about 30o (see Figure 2.19),

The handle. The handle of a saw is generally made of kiri wood (paulownia) or ho
(poplar), Pieces with a minimum of warpage or cracks are selected. The weight of the saw
handle should balance the weight of the saw blade. Two identical, grooved pieces are glued
together, as shown in Figure 2.20, Then the end where the body is inseried is securely
wrappod with rattan. The tang of the blade may be held in place with pine tar, but it is not
glued.
Figure 2.20 The saw handle (before being assembled).
Care of the saw
Sharpening the teeth. When used properly, saw teeth need sharpening less frequently
than Wostern saws. When they do, however, in Japan they are generally brought to a professional sharpener, since this is a task which requires great skill and exacting precision,
The teeth are sharpened with a special sharpening f ile called a tpather tile (Figure 2.21). The
body of the sa'w is pressed up tightly against a block of wood slightly larger than a common
post, and the teeth are sharpened one at a time, as illustrated ln Figure 2.22. Often a sharpening vice is used, as shown in Figure 2.23,

The blade, The blade is made from two types of steel. The back is of soft steel, and the
front, a very hard, high-carbon sleel. ln this way, the strength and resilience of the softer
steel combine with the edge-holding capability of the harder steel to meet the special requirements of this tool.
lf the steel is too hard, the blade will chip easily, on the other hand, il the ste€l is too solt,
the blade will not hold an edge. Because of this, when acquiring a plane, it is imponant to
fifst consult with someone who knows tools. Evidence of handwork, such as carbon specking or an unevenness at the line where the two pieces of steel are laminatod together, is
often an indication ot a high-quatity blade.
Figure 2.38. The blade.
softwood is easier to plane with harder steel. The planing quality is also affected by the
angle at which the blade is set in the plane body. To plane hardwoods or extremely hi(]hly
figured woods, 45-55o is the most'efficient angle, and for softwoods, 30-oso is ideal. Very
soft woods, such as krn (paulownia) or balsa, may require 22o*ooo, Most North American
woods work well at an angle of g2o-3so. Generally, the harder the wood, the steeper the
angle required to plane it. some denser woods, such as maple or exotic hardwoods, may
require a blade angle as steep as ss-600, Through experience, a craftsman will learn the
proper angle lor use with each type ef wood.
SETTING SLOPE
Flgure 2.39. Angle of blade tip.
Tools and Thelr Gare lg
The single-blade plane, shown in Figure 2.40, is used for finishing. The mouth of its blade
must be kept at a minimum in order to not tear the grain, This is a very difficult plane for the
layman to use,
The rough and medium planes, with their larger mouth, have a tendoncy to bite into the
wood's surface. A wider mouth means that the pressure of the gliding surface is farther
away from the cutting surface of the blade; this allows more wood to be lifted or torn before
being sheared or cut off, To a certain sxtent, this can be reduced by adjusting the throat
opening betwoen the gliding suriace of the block and the cutting edge of the blade, To prevent torn grain, a chipper blade is used (see Figure 2.41). This type of plane is called a
double-blade plane. The edges of the blade and the chipper blade are placed back to back,
as shown in Figure 2.42. Dimension a, shown there, should be .020 inch for a rough plane,
.013 inch for a medium plane, and ,010 inch * for a finlshing plane. The chipper blade should
r, t be advanced beyond the cutting edge, or shavings will clog the blade.

The plane body. Hardwood, being more durable, is preferred in making the plane body.
Heartwood (the portion without knots) of li/hite or red oak are good choices. Wood from the
root end of a tree and the wood near knotd should not be used because these tdnd to warp,
The outsido-of-the-tree side of the wood should be used for the bottom face oj a plane, so
that any warpage will occur at the edges and be more easily corrected. The side of the
wood closer to the root should be used for the head of a plane. Tho grain of the block will
then run more smoothly upon the grain of the board being planed. The direction of movement should be with the graln itself and nol against the grain pattern of the block.

Correcting blade gap, The cutter blade and chipper blade of a Japanese plane must be
perfectly flat. This is essential to its proper functioning. When these are not precisely
matched, shavingls collect in the space between them and will very quickly close the mouth
of the plane, To correct this, sharpen both blades, and adjust lho unequal protuberance on
the back of the chipper blade by pounding it with a hammer against an anvil.
To chock whether the blades are flat, place the blades together in the position shown in
Figure 2,46a. Noxt, the fingers are placed on the lettered positions shown in Figure 2.46b;
the thumb on a, the index finger on b, the middle linger on q, and the li{tle tinger on d. Then
apply pressure on each corner separately as shown in Figure 2A@. ll you hear a click, the
blades are still not flat, and the chipper blade should be adiusted.

AdJustlpg the plane body
There is a legend in which two boards planed by Hidari Jingoro adhered lo each other when
placed together. This happened because lhe two planed surfaces were so perfectly flat that
air could not enter between them. Accurate planing depends on the proper care of the plane
body. Adjusting the body of the plane entails checking the shape of the sole with a straight
edge and adjusting the bottom surface of th€ body lor easier planing. Make certain the
blade is retracled into the block before commencing. The straight edge should be placed in
the posilions indicated in Figure 2.47, and the sole checked for flatness by holding it at eye
level (Figure 2.48). The surface can then be corrected by pcraping with a toothing plane, as
shown in Figure 2.49. Check first the throat and then the front or nose, since flatness in all
other directions should be based upon the relationship of these two gliding surfaces.

Exporience will teach the proper angle lor use in cutling difterent woods. A harder wood
needs a sleeper angle to have more material supporting the cutting edge, Softor wooqs

The parts ot tho chlsel
The parts of a chisel are shown in Figure 2.55. The chisel blade is made of two pieces ol
steel, a softer steel at the back with harder steel laminaled on the lop and bent around to
form the cutting edge. The softer steel absorbs the shock ol hammer blows; the tang inserted into the handle is part of this piece, The handle is usually made of red or while oak or
other hardwood. At the top, a crown ring (or terrule), which is inserted about t4 inch from the
struck end the handle, prevents the handle trom splitting when struck by a hammer.
It is very important that the handle and blade ol a chisel be aligned. Otherwise, it is dilficult
lo sonso ho'W one is cutting.

Sharpenlng Stones
ln daily use, culting edges become dull or may chip, and must be sharpened, Thls can be
done anywhere that is well lit and free ot dust, since a carpenter's job site is not fixed,
blades are often sharpened outside, away from direct sunlight which will dry the stone.
However, those who work indoors, such as millworkers and cabinetmakers, should have a
counter whore they c.an sharpen blades. The counter should be construcled so that the top
face of the sharpening stone will be slightly below waist height when placed on the counter.
Or, ono may kneel bslore the stone placed on a low bench or level board. ln both cases, the
working height should be adjusted so that balance and flexibility in the arms and shoulders
are optimal.
The types of sharpening stones and their functions
sharpening stones are classified as coarse, medium, and finisiy stones, and are used in sequence when sharpening blades, Japanese sharpening stones should be used with water
only' never oil' The water lubricates the action of the stone and flushes away the motal
rubbed from the blade. Manmade stones should be soaked in water lor a tew minutos be.
fore each use, but natural stones must never be soaked.
A rough manmade stone made of silicon carbide, aluminum carbide, or aluminum silicate
is used to remove chips from blades. The coarse stone, made of sandstone, is used to begin any sharpening praclice except daily maintenance, in which only lh€ medium and finish
stones are used. The medium stone is made of shale and is dark blue in color. when used,
it follows lhe coarse stone, after which the blade is applied to the finish stone.
There are manmade finish stones and naturally formed ones, The tinest edges are obtained lrom the natural stones, Manmade stones are oflen too soft and, genorally speaking,
the harder a finish stone is, the sharper the edge it will produce, The best Japanese stones
are Honyama stonos, lound near Kyoto. They are beige in color and tond to be quite expensive, especially the larger ones. Better natural stones have an even texture and no inclusions of gril or other foreign matter in the sharpening surface.
When a blade has been sharpened on a medium or coarse slone, a bun of metal appoars
on the front side of the blade. This "wire edge" can be lelt by running a finge/along the top
side of the blade edge ofl the front. Do not break this off; removo it with the next finest
stone, The finish stone will not leave a wire edge.

28 Toolr and Thelr Garo
usrng lhe modlum stone, Even after tho blade has been sharpened on a rough or
coarse etone, the cutting surlace will still appear dull, and needs to be sharpened on the medlum stone to docrease lhe roughness and straighton the edge' The blade is sharpened on
the modium stone until it has luster and a finer wire edge,
IJsing the fine finish stone. The finish stone is used to remove the wire edge and to polish the smoothed edge still further. A$ shown in Figure 2.62a., Ihe back side ot the blade is
run back and forth Several times with slight pr6$sure to remove the wire edge. Then the
angled pofiion ot the blade is polished repeatedly (Figure 2,62h). These steps are repeated
untll the wire edge is removed, gradually deCreasing pre$sure and increasing the number ot
strokes as the final finishing is approached,
As described above, the wire edgp can be felt with the fingers, but it should not be forcefully removed by them, Since this *lll t"ut away parts oi the cutting edge. When the wire
edge has been successfully removed, the whit€ gleam will no longer be visible when the
btade's front face is retlecting light at eye level, Finally, wipe the blade with camellia oil or
any refined or non-coagulating oil, and store it.
Figure 2,62. Finishing the cutting edge
Sharpenlng technlque
Posture. The single most important factor in sharpening is balance' The surface on
which blades are to be sharpened should be lower than the waist, and one's posture should
allow lor free, horizontal mOvements ol the arms. Never assume a posture with the
shouldor at thelcenter ol the motion, resulting in roundod movements of the arm, for this will
causo the bladb's edge lo be sharpened to a curve. The posture must provide for a horizontal rnovernent with even pressure lrom the {ingers on the blade. (See Figure 2 63.)
Tools and Their Gare Zg
Pressure to sharpen tho blade is applied only on the push stroke; on the return, the angle
of the blade to the stone is retained but without pressure. Each stroke will measure about 6
to 7 inches, extending over the full {lat length of the stone. The blade is held in both hands to
steady it. A slight flex in the wrists will help to maintain a constant angle through the length
of the stroke. A steady, slight "wavsrt or ripple in the wator in advance of the blado as it is
pushed foward is a good indication of a smooth, even stroke in which the proper angle has
been maintained. The consistent sound ol metal-to-stone contact is another key to proper
technique.
lf one is rushed, or the mind wanders, or the heart is not in it, the blade cannot be sharpened accurately. The blade must be sharpened with complete concentration for ten to fifteen minutes, As one becomes proficient, a blade can be sharpened in just a lew minutes.
HORIZONTALMOTION 
ROUNDEDMOTION
oooo 
POOR
Figure 2.63. Sharpening technique.
The surtace of the sharpening stone. The surlace on which the blade is to be sharpened
must be perfectly flat, However, the front and back ends of the coarse and modium stones
should have a slight bevel, as shown in Figure 2.64. To obtain and maintain this stope, the
blade should be pushed over the front end once every three times it is pushed tonruard, The
lront and back ends ol the stione should be reversed after using it, so that the front becomes
the back, lf this is consistently done, the proper bovel will always be maintained. The entire
flat surface ol the stone should be used evenly, $o that a depression is not created in the
center of the sharpening surface. lf deprossions or distortions appear on the stone's surface, the cutting edge of the blade cannot be sharpened to a straight line, To remove distortion or depressions from the surface of the stone, rub the water-soaked surJace back and
forth on a hard, flat surJace suah as concrote until the stone is flat. lf concrete alone does
not work well enough, add some sand onto the surface of the concrete, and repeat the rubbing.
"""' iiiTri;"1,P'Ti.1 "o"
Figure 2,64. Sharpening through the bevel,

## S. 22

30 Tools and Their Gare
From time to time, fresh water must be added to the surface of the stone, to prevent a
build-up of metal paste, formed by the grindings from the blade and stone, which will make it
difficult to determine the accuracy of the sharpening stroke, as well as leading to uneven
contact between blade and stono. This water should be kept clean to avoid the possibility of
contamination. ln order to prolong the stone's life, a wooden base should be made for it'
making sure that the inside-o{-th€-tree side of the board faces up. The stone may be inset
into this base but should not be glued to it, since warpago or conlraction of tho wood block
could craCk the stone. Natural stOnes are susceptible to waler damage, so all sides except
the sharpening surface should be coated with paint or paper to restrict absorption.
After sharpening is complete, all impurities should be wiped or washed from tho surface
of the stone, and the stone should be dried, out of sunlight, belore being stored.
Other Tools
Awls or glmletg
Awls, by their twisting action, can pierce holes in wood. Pilot holes are made with an awl
(Figure 2.65a) to prevent nails from slipping and boards from splitting They are also used
for making holes through which wires and oth6r thin objects are to pass (Figure 2'65b); as a
countersint< for recessing tho head of a nail or screw (Figure 2.65c); and for boring still
deeper and larger holes (Figure 265d). They are extremely handy in awkward positions and
in locations where an electric drill or brace and bit is inconvenient'

Tools and Thelr Gare 3l
Hammers
All Japanese quality hammers are laminated just like the other tools. They havo a very hard,
tempered steel lace and a mild steel body. The genno (Figure 2.66) is a special hammer
with two faces which appear, at first glance, to be the same. However, one face is flat and
the other, slightly convex. The flat lace of the genno is used to drive large nails. The other
end, with its slightly rounded face, is used with chisels and is used to "kill the wood". Whon,
for instance a mortise and tenon joint is made very tigft, in order to be able to introduce the
pieces without too much stress on them, the wood (thb tenon piece) is pounded very carefully with this roundod face of the genno, depressing the surface of the wood. During lhe
next six to twelve hours, after tho tonon has been inserted, the compressed wood returns to
ils original size, coming up very tight. Care must be taken to used the rounded face of the
genno, lor killing the wood with the flat face would cut the fibers and ruin it.
the kanazuchi (Figure 2.67) is used for driving small nails, and its pointed end can be
Lsed as a set, as well as for making pilot holes in rough surfaces. Traditionally, it was used
for the latter purpose by roofers to make holes in shingles. As a rule, the size and weight ol
hammer used should be in relationship to the size of the work to be done.
Figure 2.66. Genno hammer
Figure 2.67. Kanazuchi hammer
A Word on Machlne Tools
Power tools are ol extreme value to the Japanese carpenter in removing, or hogging out,
large quantities of wood. While machine tools can rarely match handwork in versatility and
acute precision, much time and labor can be saved by employing them wherever they can
be used without decreasing the quality of the work.

CHAPTER 3
GHARACTERISTICS OF WOOD
Understanding tJ\rood
Wood is alive, Even aiter trees have been cut, wood continues to respond to environrnental
conditions. Before, during, and after construction, changes in the wood's characteristics
can have serious consequences for the integrity of a structure. With proper understanding,
mishaps can be {oresoen and avoided.*
Selectlng \ltdood
Wood for use in any structure should be properly seasoned. Most lumber today is kiln dried,
a process which bakes the cells on the outer layer of a board, damaging their plasticity,
Often the wood beneath this surface layer has a higher moisture aontent. Become lamiliar
with your wood suppliers, whether they be lumber yards or custom mills. The methods they
use in seasoning wood will aifect its performance when it is used in a structure,
Because wood is so susceptible to its environment, one should allow it sufficient time to
adjust to the climate in which it is to be used. This is especially true {or wood imported from
another country or another region. lf possible, lumber, particularly structural members,
should be stored close to the site on which they are to be used for a year or more, in order
to allow them to bdjust to seasonal variation in temperature and humidity.
*For an excellent trealment of the sub.iect, see R. Bruce Hoadley, Underslanding Wood: A
Craftsman's Guide to Wood Technology (Newtown, cr: Taunton Press, 1980).

species, such as cedar or cypress, each with its own characteristics, A workman should become lamiliar with the different woods available which have the features deslred for each
use, one good way to identify local woods which are suitable for use in wood trame construction is to visit local heritage buildings. The craftsmen who built them were generally

Storlng Finished Lumber

selected to store lhe wood. The roof and encrosure shourd be buirt according to need, and
lumber should be stacked in a sloped pile, in an orderly manner.

The Molsture Conlent ol Wood
By and large, all Warpage, expansion, contraction' swelling, and phrinkage in *ooo i[ a

Using Hearlwood to Advantage
When a board is split in half through the heart center, the two pieces will often bend like a
bow (Figure 3.4). This is particularly true in a log which has been partially dried. The sapwood, having a higher moisture content, tends to have a higher rate of contraction than the
heartwood. This phenomenon can be used to good advantage in obtaining shaped lumber
for curved structures.
Even a badly warped piece, as long as il is not cracked, may be saved tor a use which requires a bend or curve. ln Japan, wood for braces is often taken lrom the part of the tree at
which a branch meets the trunk. The natural curvature of its grain makes for greater strength
in this use
Figure 3,4. Lateral warpage.
Warpage
The most common distortions in lumber are shown in Figure 3.5. The outside-otthe-tree
side of a board will tend to become concave, while the inside-of-thetree side of the board
will tend to bedome convex. ln the past (and still Somewhat today) this tendency was
counteracted by leaving cut logs to soak in the water at the mill for as long as one or two
years, during which time the sap and water interact and the sap is removed. Lumbor leJt in
water dries more evenly, and i6 more stable and easier to work. Wood used for cabinetry'
sculpture, and furniture, where distortion cannot be permitted, must be properly air dried or
kiln dried at low temperature lor a long period after soaking to considerably reduc€ the
moisture content.

Correctlng warpage
Tho concave face of a warped plank (Figure 3.6a) is difficult to plane because the center
area is lower than the edges. with thin boards, the warpage can be temporarily corrected by
pouring water on the concave face of the board, as shown in Figure 3.6b. The soaked
board should then be placed upside down on a flat surface, arid in a {ew minutes the board
will be ready for praning. However, never prane a sogoy board, since the wet fibers wiil brnd,
preventing a smooth finish, and the wetness will damage the plane blade and body,
warpage in a beam or other larger member can be reduced by cutting a shallow saw k€rt
along the length of the board on a side which will not be exposed wnen tre bullding ls completed. small wedges should then b€ inserted to keep the kerf open, Forces whlch coulcl re_
sult in warping or checking will tend to be absorbed by the kerf.
ab

Figure 3.6. Correcting warpage.
\tlorklng wlth warpage ln wood
The outsides of floor boards should be placed facing upward, as shown in Figure 3.7. The
grain on the outside of the board is finer, its paftern is more pronounced, and it overlaps and
will not rise, so that even when the floor is wiped it witt not splinter. with use and the passage of time, the force ol people walking on a board used in this manner will tend to flatten
the warpage without cracking the board.

Defects ln Wood
I
t
The most common detect in wood is the knot*the cross-section of a branch appearing on
the board surface. There are two types of knots: dead, where ths wood in the knot has dried
out more than the surrounding board and wiil often drop out of the knot hore, and rive,
where the grain of the knot is simpry an imperfection in the grain of the board, rf at a[ pos_
sibre, they both shourd be avoided, since knots decrease the strength of the materiar and
contribute to instability and the likelihood of the wood warping. Joints should not be cut in a
knot or knot hore, The denser wood of the knot wil darnage toor brades, and the unstabre
grain leads to weakness in the loinery.
other common defects are sprits, burrs, pitch pockets, and ingrown bark. Their pres.nce
should be permitted only where structural soundness and estheflcs will not be adversely affected. rn sorne feature boards or detair work, the variabre grain of such defects can rend a
ploaslng appearance to such pleces as paneling or cabinet doors.
Tleatment ol the Lumber
ln traditionar construction, wood is used in its naturar form. weathering of exterior boards
adds ruster to the naturar grain and coror of the wood. Vl/ood stains or paints are seldom
used rf circumstances require it to prevent soir, termite, or other insect damage, as weil as
dry rot due to moisture, the wood may be treated,
Methods of Treatment
ln the course of construction
ing dirty or discolorod.
Oils
two methods are used to prevent finlshed pieces from becom-
I
After the finishing praning, before the wood can become soired, it is rubbed with a transparent oil' Any smudges can then be easily wiped off. The oil should be rubbed onto the wood
with a croth wrapped around a cotton balr. cameilia oir, made frorn the berries of the brack
camellia bush, is often used for lhis purpose, as are some oils derived from seaweed, once
the structure is compretely assembred, the oir may be washed or wiped off.
Paper or cloth wrapplng
In strong sunshine naturar woods wiil burn and their surfaces wiil discolot. To prevent this,
boards for interior use are wrapped with paper or croth after they havp been cut, praned, and
chiselled. This wrapper is rater removed when construction is comprpted. rn the case of wail
members, it is nol removed until mud or plaster has been applied.

40 Ghataoteristics ol Wood
Wood llnlshes
ln most traditional Japanesb structures, paints, varnlshes, or othel tinishes are seldom
usod, except for somg decorative purposes in temples, or occasiona|ly to prevent moisture
penetration on, lor exdmple, beam ends. coal tar or creosote may be applied to the base of
posts or other lgotings to resist water damage, one method ol linishing sometimes used is
scorching with a torch of logs or posts, This seals olt the cells of a porous grain and also
darkens and highlights the grain pattern.
Termltes
since termites will enter the wood at the cut ends and eat their way up along the pitch, the
cut ends should not be set directly on soil or concrete. The ends should be protected as
shown in Figure 3.8, wrapped with a metal sheet, or placed on a wood base in such a way
that the end grain is not tacing the g[ound, Then, the protected end or base should be set
upon a rock or concrete slab, never in soil. ln cases where damage is anticipated deepito
procautions, a short piec€ may be Joined, using a splicing ioint (kanawa tsugl), to the end of
a post, This piece may then be removed and replaced should damago occur'
since termite damage will not be visible on the exterior surtaco, careful inspection is necessary, when a structure is harboring termites, a hollow sound ls produced when eaten
wood ls tapped; in this way the damaged areas can be detected'
some species of cedar and cypress have a natural insecticide which discourages
tormit€s and other insects from entering the wood'
METAL SHEE
Figuro 3.8. Protecting the post from moisture.
Characteristics of Wood 4l
Another way to protect lumber is to paint or soak it with creosote, but this process cannot
be used in shiraki zukuri (natural wood) constluction.
l
Moisture and Ventilation
Where there is considerable moisture, or poor ventilation which will prevent the lumber from
drying, rot due to bacteria will develop and gradually weaken the structure. lf a waterproofing solution is applied to the base members of the structure up to 3 feet from the ground,
this will prevent moisture from entering into the wood,
Other Conslderations
The primary source ol damage to wood construction is fire. A second problem results lrom
the increased use oJ stucco as an'exterior Jinish, Stucco serves as an exlerior skin which
decreases ventilation and causes dry rot. A third cause ol damage to wood construction
has an economic basis: heart wood is used less frequently in order to reduce construction
costs, Lastly, construction work is no longer being done with suflicient care, as it once was,
For reasons such as these, the wooden buildings constructed before Wofd War ll had a
lile expectancy ol one hundred years, while those built after the war have an expectancy of
only about twenty years. in addition to the practices discussed, with the timely repair of
leaks, waterproofing, and regular maintenance, buildings can be cared for to make them
last longer.

LAYOUT
Layout reters to drawing on the lumbor the cuts that will be made in order to asemble the
building. The implements used by Japanese woodworkers lor doing layout work are the ink
marker (sumlsashl) and ink container (sumltsubo).
lnk Marklngs (Sumlzuke)
lnk applied with a bamboo ink marker (sumisashl) is preferable to pencil for a number of
reasons: it makes a finer, more accurate line; it doesn't get dull nearly as quickly as a pencil;
it leaves only a surfaco mark, rather than penetrating and cutting the wood as a pencildoes;
and it is highly visible and will not rub off.
The lnk Conlalner (Sumltsubo)
The lnk containor consists of a pot which holds ink, a long string with which to mark out
lines, a wheel on which to rewind the string, ink-saturated balls of raw silk or cotton through
which the string is pulled when it is rewound, and a pin or hook at the end ol the string for
securing it in place (see Figure 4.1). Though these cgntainers may vary slightly in their

Uslng the lnk line and contalner (sumltsubo)
The wood should always be marked with a center line, which is then used as the reference
from which measurements are taken. This practice is essential when using logs. The ink Iine
and container (surnifsubo) is used for this, as shown in Figure 4.6a. First the hook or pin is
stuck into the center of one end of the timber. Then the string, soaked with ink, is pullod to
the center ol the other end. The string is pulled taut and held as far away from the pin as the
arm can comfortably reach (see Figure 4,6b), lt is pulled upward 2-4 inches then released
so that the inked string hits the surlace of the board, marking it with a clear line, Caro should
be taken to maintain the proper consistency in the ink, so that it is fluid enough to leavo a
mark, but not so liquid as to drip or spray when the string is snapped.
Figure 4.6. Marking a line with the sumitsubo.

When drawing oblique lines for the lsuglte (end joints) and the shlguchl (right angle joints), if
the degree of obliqueness is kept consla t throughout, fewer mistakes will be ;nade when
drawing out the male and female joint parts, Figures 4.9 and 4,.10 give examples of the oblique lines for dovetail, gooseneck, and other joints using the width on the carpenter's square
scale. The carpenter's square is 5 bu wjde, which corresponds to a 15 mm mortising chisel.
As illustrated below, the reference point set in making a joint ha$ to be changed in relation
to the lype 01 joint, but if the width ol the carpentor's square is used as a constant, or as a
unit by using its multiples, the work becomes simpler. The slope angle of the joint will vary
according to use (1 in 5 or 1 in B is common Jor a dovetail). Using reference points and the
constant width of the carpenter's square allows the an{te to be more easily replicated on
both the male and the female pieces.

Board drawlnge
BlLroprints are inconvenient at a job site because they tear easily and are damaged by wind
and rain. For this reason, board drawings are u$ed. These are ink drawings showing the
prodominant structural aspecls of a house (usually the floor plan), on thin plywood or other
similar broard, ordinarily, floor plans and structural prans are simply drawn, and grids with
numbers and letters are assigned, as shown in Figure 4,11,
Tho grid letters and numbers identify the location and directional orientation of $tructural
members such as posts, beams, girders, and footings. starting at the bottom reft corner of
the floor pran, letters (A), (B), (c) . , up and numbers (1), (2), (3) . . . across, are assigned to
the grid, with the side with the entry way closest to the viewer, With those symbols, a column
located at the intersection of grid lines c and 3 can be identified as corumn c3. Each grid
marking is spaced to represenl intervals of B feet.

CHAPTER 5
WORKII{G THE WOOD
Good work cannot be produced unress the work is enjoyabre; making a thing shourd bring
pleasure, not paln. The work must never be rushed to completion, but done with care and
patience. Above ail, one should trury enjoy one's work. rt is arso rmportant to remember that
wood is a riving thing-one must understand its proporties and not oppose them. whenever
work is forced, it either ruins the piece, damages the toors, or rnjures the worker, so work
must always be done with the utmost care.
Sawlng
ln this section, sawing method will be explalned.
Japaneso saws are made to cut when puiled, The pushing is done without force, but force
ls applied in pulling the saw. Good sawing require.."ol'.i.i"nt *r,nn',n,i"." tJrw"ra ano
backward movements.
lf a work bench is ro be used, it shourd be at about hip height, otherwise, saw horses are
placed on the ground and the materials are sawed on them.

Sawlng posture
(1) As shown in Figure 5,1 , the piece to b6 cut is steadied by the lelt hand, and the cut line itsolf ls sel in kont of the right hand. When sawing small pieces a work table is used, while
larger pleces such as framing members are cut on saw horses. Thus the height at which the
lurnber is cut will vary, but the pulling force is more easily applied at a lower position. When
using a saw on a work table, the wood is held firmly in place with the left hand, and the saw
is pulled with the right hand, Large pieces are cut by securing them with the left foot and pulling the saw with both hands.
(2) ln one-handed sawing, the grip is such that lorce is applied using the thumb of the right
hand on top of the handle, aligned with the direction ol the cut, while the forefinger acts as a
lulcrum underneath. The other fingers stabilize the saw, using a light grip, (For left-handed
people, this is reversed.)
With larger saws, both hands are used, The right hand firmly grips the saw's handle near
Its butt, while the left hand clasps |he handle lightly near the nock (see Figure 5.2). While
pulling, force is applied with tne rigflt hand; while pushing, the force is decreased. Little force
ls required in comparison to using a Western saw, since the weight of the saw blade, which
is wider and heavier near the tip, assists the cut. lf sawing is done in this manner, there will
be less damage to the saw teeth, less fatlgue, and the cut will be more accurate, Figure 5.3
and 5.4 show the correct stance for cutting a larger pieco with a rip and crosscut saw.

with smailer saws, onry one hand, the right for right-handed peopre, is used, The grip for
one-handed sawing is adjusted to balance tho saw relative to the thickness of the piece being cut. when cutting a smaller piece, the handle is gripped nearer to the blade for more
conkol. With a larger piece, the grip is farther back on the handle and the stroke is longer.
(1) At times sawing becomes dirficurt because when the saw is cutting into the wood it goes
lurther in than harf the depth of the saw. This occurs most frequenily when cutting wood with
irregular grain (atezal). Before cutting, the grain is in a balanced conditionf but-when sucrr
wood is cut, the grain is severed, and the side with fibers having more force pres$es against
tho saw. r'his phenomenon, whore sawing becomes raborious, is cailed tsuru. wh6n sawing
wood that is thicker than the width of th€ saw, cutting becomes very difficurt, especiaily
when ripsawing, At such times, pound a wedge into the mouth of the incision to hold the cut
open for easier sawing (see Figure 5.6). When ripping longer boards, it is advisable to tilt
the board srightry, wirh the end away from you erevated, rn this way, gravity and th€ tap€r of
the brade ard the cut of tho saw more directry, puiling the teeth against th6 wood to b6 cut.

when starting to saw (especially with a rip saw) the first cut is difticult to make at the exact
location desired, because the saw tends to get caught in the lumber, To make the first incision at the desired rocation, prace the rhumb next to it, bend the thumb, pracing the saw
against the thumb joint and its teeth at the desired rocation, as shown in Figure u ulr"n;" ou
making smalr sawing movements whire the saw remains against the rhumb, This pro.Jorr"
for maklng the initial cut is called hrkrk omi. Hikikata is the term which describes the main actions of sawing. As described above, continue to saw with care and consistency, using the
honba (teeth rlear the handle).
Hikiowari describes the correct manner of sawing when nearing the end of the cut. rf
hikikata were continued untir the end, the wood being cut would break, spritting the end or
the piece, or erse the force appried wourd become un-controilabre, canying the saw into the
saw horse, into the ground, or into one's crothes. rnstead, when nearing tnJ eno ot tne cut, it
is important to srow down the sawing, hording the piece being severed with one hand, and
continuing to saw genlly until the cut is completed.
when cutting through a piece, after a shailow cut has been made on the first side, rotare
the piece away from you and continue cutting. The saw cut always advances toward you.
continuing this procedure untir the piece is cut through allows a clean cut to be made, without splintering. Unlike western sawing procedure, the piece is supported on both sides of
the cut, whether on a bench or on saw horses, so that the cut end wir not fail, risk;;;;

Planlng
Whether a carpenter can do a good job of planing or not can largely depend on the quality
of tho board being planed, To plane well, all conditions, including the sharpness ol the
t.rlade, proper height of support, and the others menlioned previously must be satistied.
Since the care of the blade and body have already been discussed, only the planing itself is
oxplained here.
Figure 5.7, The ends of the tree.
Worklng with diflerent types of wood
I
ln order to plans properly, the charactoristics of the wood must lirst be known, Even with a
top quality plane th€ planing cannot be done successfully without first determining il the surlace to be planed is the f ront or back side, or whether it is an alezai. The difference between
a prolessional and an amateur is the ability to distinguish between them.
Tha kiura (back) and kiomote (front) sides are shown in the drawings below. The base of
tlro troo is called moto (butt), and the top of the tree is called sue or sueguchi (tip) (see Figr,rro 5.7), When squared lumber is cut lrom a log (see Figure 5.8), the side closer to the
zaislrlrr (pith) is the klura (back side), and the side further away from it is the klomole (front
riitltr). Oll carefully inspecting the grains on the front side they are seen to run up from the
Working the Wood 57
*'@'ffi
'p 
Figure 5.8. sides of the board,
butt end to the tip. Examination of the overlapping grains shows that they grow outward as
they overlap the pith, On the back side the grain runs in the opposito direction, from the out'
side towards the center, with the grain closest to the center at the very top The direction of
planing is determined by these conditions. The planing directions are called either oime or
sakame. The kiomote (front side) of a board should be planed from the tip to the butt, and
the back side should be planed from the butt to the tip' This is called oime planing (Figure
5.9), while planing in the opposite (wrong) direction is called sakame (Figure 5,10), When
wood is planed in lhe sakame direction, the plane blade cuts into the grain and gouges out
bits of the surface, The surface can never be planed Smoothly in this way, To correct such a
Surface, the kasaneba (chipper blade) mentioned above, has to be used,
planing should be in the oime direction, but there remains the problem of knots. Knots are
cross-sections of branches and are always present in lumber With pith. The grains around
the knot run in dilferent directions and thus the direction of planing becomes inconsistent. At
such times, the planing direction will have to become sakame' Furthermore, sinco knots
grow towards the tip, the planing ol knots must be done towards the tip as well. This type of
planing is called oifushi.
When a hard knot is planed with sakame, the cutter's edge may chip. Since the direction
of the plane cannot be changed at each knot, the blade should be exposed as little as possible, with th€ tightest throat opening possible, and a chipper blade should be used. Always
plane carefully and tenaciou$ly at knots,

Figure 5,10, Planing sakame (wrong)'

Figure 5,12. Planing sequence.
As shown in Figure 5.11, the head of the cutter is held very gently with the left hand cupped
agalnst it, while the body of the plane is grasped lirmly with the right hand about half-way between the butt end of the body and the cutter. Next, as shown in Figure 5.12, put the weight
of your body on tho left leg and stretch forward, With both arms stretched well forward, but
remaining well balanced and under control, pull the plane back, at the same time extending
the right leg straight back and transferring your weight onto the right leg-all in one motion.
A piece is planed by repeating this movement and stepping back until the end is reached.
Most important is to keep the plane always basically parallel to the piece being planed and
to qpply pressure consistently throughout the length to be planed (see Figure 5.13). Some
I
,i
(a) coBFEcrPoslrloN
Working the Wood 59
carpenters hold the plane at an angle to the wood, Though pulling becomes easier due to
the decreased lriction between plane stock and surface, the work is made more dilficult later
while using the finishing plane, when the surlace cannot be planed absolutely llat. When the
material requires only rough finishing, the plane may be held at an angle, but this is not a
good practice.
When planing a wide piece, move the plane towards you after each movement. After the
whole width has been planed, start over again from the side away from you, alter stepping
back, as shown in Figure 5.14. The final pass should be one continuous stroke.
Figure 5.14. Planing a wide board
Figure 5.15, Preparing the board end.
Before starting to plane, shave the end of the piece with a chisel, as shown in Figure 5,15,
to remove any sand or particles which may cause the plane blade to chip. To plane knots
with $ap or with a sheen, soak the board in water lor about 3 to 5 minutes to solten, because such a board surlace consists of f ibers running in different directions which will cause
the blade to chip, One must work hard to plane such boards flat. After planing in this fashion, dry your tool, to prevent rusting of the blade or warpage of the block.
To plane producing shavings of a constant width which match the width qf the cutter, and
which are of continuous length from the beginning to the end of the cut, is considered a sign
of the highest skill in planing,
At the end of the work always recess the blade into the body of the plane, and lay the
plane on its side-not on.its sole,

To measure the depth of a hole that has been chiseled' lnsert a chisel or small stick perpendicularly into the hole. Locate the top ol the hole on the stick with the fingers and re'
move the stick from the hole. Then measure the depth with a ruler, when chiseling a hole
right through a board, roughly chlsel to about half the depth then chisel from the other side'
When the hole has been made, a square or a similar tooi is used to check the verticality and
Smoothness o1 the cut surface. as shown in Figure 5,25' Then the hole is squared and
smoothed to finish it
Figure 5.25, Checking squareness of a hole'
Another practical method for making holes is to use a brace or gimlet as shown in Figure
5.26. By roughly boring a hole of the required size and depth with a brace or gimlet' and
then squaring and tinishing it with a chisel, a hole can be made qulte easily

Hatsurl-GhlPPing
/Jolsrr, ls the method used for removing a thick plece oi wood by chipping most of it out' ln
ntcltjern times, due to lumber mills producing completed pieces, hatsuri work has become
llmitod to special construction and log work. But as hatsurl work is also done when correctil]0 warpage and otherdistortions, abasic explanation is provided' lnhatsuri work, achona
(rrrizo) and an ono (hatchet) are generally used (Figures 5.27 and 5 29)'
cRoss.sEctoN %
Figure 5.27. Chona (adze|.
Worklng the lilood 67
Chona-adze
An adze is used to trim and shape surfaces, such as in carving out a portion of a post surface, making a flat surlace on a log, providing a llat area on akoya bari (roof beam) for the
koya tsuka (end post), for end joint surfaces on a nagekake bari (beam), and for V-slanted
connecting surfaces ol joints such as the daimochi tsugl, shrppasami tsugi, and okkake
daisen tsugi. The adze is worked while securing the lumber with the left foot, with the toes
extended beyond the piece as shown in Figure 5.28, and striking with the blade at a slight
angle and in front, instead ol straight into the material. This requires tremendous skill and is
difficult to do unless one has quite a lot of experience. The shavings or chips are called
koppa. Thin and long koppa rather than thick ones indicate the skillf ulness of the carpenter.
Since there are only a lew widely scattered people who can do good hatpurl work, much
of this kind ol work is done with a chisel imitating the finishing work ol an adze.
Figure 5,29, Ono (hatchet)
Ono - hatchet
The hatchet is used to split wood. This is easily achieved by striking a piece and relying on
its characteristic of splitting when it is struck parallel to the grain, The hatchet is also used to
sharpen lhe ends of pegs, to trim the corners of squared pieces, and to shape logs.
Woodworklng Procedures
The pieces are shaped in the tollowing sequences:
(1) Select manufactured square lumber.
(2) Place a level at each end of the piece, as shown in Figure 5.30, then look at the tops of
the levels to see it they align. lf they do not align, the wood is warped. ln this case, plane it,
removing the warp difference gradually, until the pieoe is flat. Next, using the surnllsubo,
mark a length of 4 inches from each end ol the piec4.

CHAPTER 6
THE FUNGTIOTS OF
VARIOUS MEMBERS
chapter 5 exprained the making of various major end joints and right angre joints as weil as
ways of working with the materials. ln this chapter, using wooden residential structures as
examples, the functions of end joints and right angle joints on major struclural and architectural members will be discussed,
There are two framing methods used in Japanese construction: okabe, in which all framing members are concealed at both the exterior and interior by the wall finishing materials
(sheet rock or lath); and shrnkabe, in which major structurar members such as posts and
beams are exposed and treated as finishing materials,

Dpdal-Mudsllls
The mudsill is the skeletai framing member which rests on the footing, ties the posts, and
transfers the load to the foundation.
(1) Materials
since mudsills are placed close to the ground, raised on a post or stone foundation, they
are susceptible to moisture and rot. Mudsills are usually made of pith wood, preferably a
reddish wood. cherry, chostnut, Jbpanese cypress, and fir, which resist moisture we||, are
ali used, but Japanese cypress and f ir are most f requently used due to their ease of milling.
(2) Measurements.
The mudsills should be large enough {or the posts to sit on the inside of the boveled edges,
as shown in Figure 6.2, Today, mudsills often are of the same dimension aslthe posts. Generally, a single story,house uses 4x4 mudsills, while a two-story house requires a larger
beam.

Hashlra-Poets
Posts are very important framing members since they serve to transfer the weight of loads
onto the ground and to support the walls. They also resist the lateral lorces ol wind and
earthquake. ln a two-story house, a single piece that goes from the ground lloor to the rool
is called atoshl bashira (a continuous post). Posts terminating within one floor are called
kuda bashira (discontinuous posts). The vertical members erected between the posts which
support the walls are called ma bashira (studs). (See Figure 6.19.)
(1) Ioshi bashlra-continuous posts.
ln the past, wooden houses with many continuous posts and with long walls were consid'
ered sturdiest. Since beams must be connected at each post when many continuous posts
are used, such posts must be kept to a minimum. Usually, continuous posts are used only
at the four corners, and Where T- or cross-shaped girders intersect.
(i) Material: Japanese cypress or cedar with pith.
(ii) Dimensions: These should be slightly larger than those recommended in Kenchiku
Kiiunho (Architectural Standards)*, and slightly bigger than the kuda bashira (disoontinuous
posts),
*Translator's note: Equivalent to the North Amerlcan Uniform Building Code.

(i) fMaterial; ln the Japanese framing method, these posts are usually exposed and are part
ol tho finished work, so they should be free of knots and pitch. They should be of either
edge-grained (quarter sawn) or flat-grained (plain sawn) wood. Usually zelkova, cherry,

poeled and polished logs are used.

square posts are used for two'story houses,
(iii) Right angle joints: The most commonly used mortise and tenon joint at both the top and
bottom ot the posts is the lan hozo (short tenon) with a pin anchor, but in places with heavy
winds, or where quality work is desired , a hozo sashl komlsen uchi is used, ll the bottom
joint fits tightly, the work of construction is made easier since the posts will not sway.

(iv) construction practice: lf a post with pith is being used lor linished work, a kerf must be

when cutting the posts, determine which are the butt and iip ends, and always place the
butt end at the bottom. Also, do not use posts with atezai (abnormal grain.)
(3) Mabashira-studs. These are membors which form and support the walls. lt must be
noted that, structurally, they are non-bearing mombers,
(i) Material: This should be wood without pith or abnormal grain, fven though studs are
non-bearing members, de nol use warped pieces.
(ii) Dimensions'. For okabe framing, the studs should be about r/s the size of the posts. For
shinkabe, studs equalling the size of the ratters are used.
(iii) Right angle joints: Atan hozo sashi joint (short tenon mortise and tenon) reinforced with
two nails is used at both the bottom and top connections to the studs.

IVokl geta-Eave Beams
The noki geta transfer the road on rhe koya bari (roof joists) to the continuous posts, as well
as connecting the ends of the posts. The noki geta also are cailed hanamoya^
(1) Material: Lumber with pith from pine, Japanese cedar, zerkova, and Japanese cypress
are used rhe most commonry used are pine, Japanese cypress, and Japanese codar.
(2) Dimensions: The wldth ol lhe nakigeta (eave beams) should match ttrat of the dodai
(mudsills). lt should also be wider than the post by the width of the bevers on both sides of
the post. The depth of the beam is determined by the rength of the span and the road it sup_
ports; usually it is anywhere lrom 1.2 to 1,6 times ils width.

Do Sashl-Subsldiary Beams
1)r,r sa.shi beams are used on the exterior walls of two-story houses. They aro placed on the
:;ocond lloor level and Serve to connect the discontinuous posts, and to Support the second
ltoor ioists and beams, These structurally important members are also known as hariuke
(troam supporters).
(l) Material: Lumber with pitch lrom pine, Japanese cypress, and Japanese cedar are used,
llrough pine and Japanese cedar are used most widely.
(2) Dirnensions: The width should matah lhat of the posts, but the depth must be determined
t)y the spacing of its supporting posts.

two aontinuous posts, when a long span requires that lwo pieces must be joined' a joint is
rrrado between them, and between two discontinuous posts. The following joints are used.
(l) Slandard construction: koshikake ari tsugi (Figure 6.3).
(rr) Quality constructioni okkake daisen lsugi (Figure 6.5).
lrr oactr case metal connectors should be used to reinlorce the ioints.
(a) Hight angle joints,
(l) l-or connection with a continuous post: Kashigi oire tan hozo sashi (oblique dado with

(ii) For connection with a corner post: Kasnlgi oire tan hozo sashi with a metal strap or metal

(5) Construction practice: When using the shlnkabe framing, the do sashi will be exposed in
the staircase and in a clerestory room. ln such cases the same care must be taken as on the
outside faces on exterior walls. lt is also important to take into account that when wood expands and contracts, causing movement, a joint tightened with wedges or keys will be
stronger than one reinforced with metal connectors.
l
Sujikai- Dlagonal Bracing
To prevont distortion of an axial frame, diagonal members aro placed between two posts.
This member is placed in such a way as to counter either compressive force or tensional
lorce. The general practice is to place the member to resist compressive force, The strength
of a diagonal brace is not determined by its size, but rather by the method of connection at
its end. Carelul atlention must therelore be given to this connection. The installation of
diagonal bracos should not be limited only to the framing ol exterior walls. lt is preJerable to
place such braces on the interior partitioning walls as well.

(1) Materials: lt is best to use lumber with pith. However, il the bracing is made by splitting a
post into thirds, then the lumber must be free of pith.
The dlagonal bracing for large buildings should be the same size as the posts. ln smaller
structures, or in shinkabe framing, the thickness should be either one half or one th jrd of the
posts.
(2) Framing
At each of the corners of a lrame, the diagonal brace is connected with the joint, ka shigi oire
(obliqued dado), secured either by a pln anchor or by large nails to both a post and a beam
(Figure 6.31).
Figure 6.31. Securing diagonal bracing
Figure 6.32. Joining intersecting braces
when intersecting with posts and studs, always notch out the posts and studs to accept
the diagonar brace. where two diagonar braces intersect, make one continuous; secure the
other halves onto it with a pin anchor or with two rarge nairs at each end (Figure 6.32).
(3) construction practice: lf the diagonal brace is connected only to a post as shown in Figure 6.33a, only the tenon acts to resist forces, For this reason, the diagonal brace should be
connected to both a post and a mudsill or beam, as shown in Figure 6.33b. since the external forces acting on a structure (i.e., wind and earthquake) are never constant, the ideal
framing practice is to install intersecting diagonal braces. However, due to wall ,raming
methods, involving various types of underlayments for different types of wall finishes, such
as stucco, board and batter, etc., there are many instances where only a single brace can
be installed, ln such cases, install the braces as shown in Figure 6.83b, balancing the left
and right sides"

Neda (or Aleda Kake)-Jolsts,
The members which run perpendicular to the obiki and which directly support the flooring
are the neda (joists). Neda kake is an obiki at the exterior wall on which the ends ol the
joists are supported.
l
(1) Materials: Theneda support a large load in relation to their size, so they are made ol
strong materials such as pine, Japanese cypress, and Douglas tir. They are eilher 2 in,
square or 172 in. x1sla in., and they are usually spaced 18 in. apart. Most neda kake are about
lVa in.x4 in. and are made of Japanese cedar. 
,

tsuke (butt) ioint or sogl tsugi (scarf) joint (Figure 6.52). The neda kake are joined at the
center line of all the posts with a tsuki tsuke joint. ln quality construction, the joint used is
watari aga kake uke, secured with two nails at each post.

used. The joists are Secured with nails on top of the neda kake (beam), or else they are connected by anosekake olre (shoulder) joint or by awatari ago kake and are all secured with
nails.

Shlla/i*Supports
Supports to which finishing materials such as floors, walls, and ceilings are attached are
called shrtali, An explanation of wooden shllali follows:.
Yuka Shltail *Subfloors
The nature of lhe finished flooring, such as falami (straw mat), vinyl tiles, flooring blocks,
sfleet flooring, and others, will determine the type of floor supports,
(1) A subfloor lor tatami is usually made of !/a in. thick pine or Japanese cedar boards
placed side by side, the ends being butt jointed and secured to the joists.with nails. ln quality
construction, the boards are also shiplapped along thoir sides.
(2) The subfloor for linoleum and vinyl or plastic tiles is usually made of Vz lo Vq in. thick
waterproofed plywood, secured to the joists with nails every 4 in. The length of the nalls
should be from 2la 2y2 timos the thickness of lhe plywood. lf the plywood were to bend as a
result of people walking over it or because of other loads, the tiles would become lobse.
Because ol this the plywood should be as thick as possible-the thicker the bettor.
(3) Subflooring for wood block floors,
The block llooring pieces are usually 9tlz to 12 in. square and % in, thick, and made of oak
or beech. The pieces are usually joined by a form ol edge joint, The subflooring for block
floors is similar to that in (2). Today there are aiso plywoods veneered with block flooring
patterns. With this type of material, the veneered plywood is placed directly over the joist in
the same way as lhe subflooring plywood in (2).
(4) Subflooring for carpeting and other covering materials.
The installation for this type of subflooring is similar to that in (2), except that here the
plywood subfloor is installed below the top surface ol the shkli (headers) and kutsuzuri
(baseboards) to allow for the thickne$s of the covering material.

Kabe Shitafi-Wall Underlaymonts or Wall Shoathlng
Among the wall underlayments are kornal (furring strlps), kizuri (tradilional wood lath), rasu
(Westernized lath) and rasu bodo (Westernized lath board) underlayment.
(1) Konai (bamboo) underlayment is the traditional Japanese lathing method. Green bamboos are sprit about 17+ in. thick and are tied to wail bridgings with strings to form a mesh.
Then clay mlxed with straw is plastered on both sides of this bamboo lath. After the application of a scratch (first) coat, smoothing (second) and a brown (third) coat, a finishing coat of
mud, sand, or plaster is applied.
Today it is common practice to put a finishing coat over the brown coat, which in turn is
applied ovor a scratch coat which has been applied over wood or metal lath.
(2) Wood lath undertayment.
wood lath is commonly made of thin, long cedar boards, about 1 za ro 1y2 in.wide and /a in.
thick, which are secured to the studs with a sright space as shown in iFigure 6.60a. The wail
is finished by applying plaster or stucco over the lath.
The boards are randomly butt-joined in groups of six to ten pieces. Laths are spaced
about % in. apart and secured to each $tud with lwo nails. At inside corners, the laths are
secured to a nailing board as shown in lFigure 6.OOb. 
l
Where tile is applied on €lther outside or inside walls, the lath board is usually about /2 in.
thick by 3 in. wide. The term which describes the lathing for tile is nakanuk'i(instead of
kizuri),

which is installed on top of the wood rath. The most commonry used finish with this type of
underlayment is sprayed integral-colored mortar over regular mortar,

Kaidan-Stalrs
ln providing vertical access, slairs must not only be placed al a convoniont location' but
consideration must be given to their design and decorative qualities. Their specifications
are spelled out in the architectural building code, and they must be built accordingly. How'
ever, as the code only SetS minimum requirements, care must be taken to build them in such
a way that they can be used comfortably lrom day to day (see Figure 6.86).

Hako kaldan-box stairs
The hako stair is walled on both sides and runs straight or is "L"-shaped. ll is very common
in residences, The stair ls composed of soku rta (stringers), fumizura (treads), uraita (sol.
fits), and at times risers (see Figure 6,87). Stalrs are usually from 30 to 32 in. wide, since
they are generally placed between posts that are 36 in, apart at their centers.
(1) Matenals: Pine, Japanese cypress, zelkova, and at timgs ruan (lauan) mahogany all
without pith are used. However, it is advisable not to uss ruan because it usually is wormqaten.
The Functlons of Varlous llllembers l4A
(2) Dimensions: Stringers and lreads should be lrcm1y4 to 13lq in. thick. The width of the
stringers should be 2 /e in. more than the height resulting f rom the pitch of the riser and tread
(see Figure 6.88). The soffits are usually made of Japanese cedar from 3/s to % in. thick, The
width is an equally divided dimension between the inside faces of the stringers. Sometimes
plywood is used for making soffits.

(3) Shiguchi -end joints.
The treads are connected to the stringers with every other tread having a kata ari suitsuki
(half dovetail dado) and the others having two kaki hozowari (split tenons) which are
secured to the stringers with wedges (see Figure 6.99),
The soffits are joined to the underside of the lreads with an ai jakkuri (shiplap) joint and
secured with nails. When the risers are installed, the soffits are removed. The risers are connected to the undersides of the upper treads with a fumita uwaba koanahori orre (dado)
joint, and secured with nails to both the stringers and the lower treads,
(4) construction practice: To prevent splinters when walking on or wiping the stairs, the
kiura (back side) of the board must always be used,

(l)Srllol level, As shown in Figure 7.16, projections of equal height are made at each end of
tlro groove. A level plane is obtained by tilling the groove with water and adjusting the tool
so that the water will cover the projection tips (points A & B).
A rnodern leveling tool has al its center a curved glass tube filled with just enough water
or cloar oll to leave a small air bubble (see Figure 7.17), By tilting the level until th6 air bublrlo ls contered, the bottom o1 tfrs tool becomes level, This level is also used in plumbing, for
pilxr installation, and in masonry construction and tile work.
