(iv) Sulungi *transit or theodolite.
A transit is comprised of a telescope, level, and level adjuster, and is set on top oJ a tripod
(see Figure 7.20), First the front leg of the tripod is set lirmly at about the center ol the work
site. The other two legs are then spread and adjusted so as to set the top aS level as possible. The transit is then attached to the tripod and is further adjusted with the leveling
screws. Since the attached level favors the higher side, two of the three leveling screws
must be turned simultaneously in opposite directions, aS Shown in Figure 7.21. Then the
tBlescope is rotated 120o and a new pair of leveling Screws are worked. This process is
repeated until the transil is set as close to perfectly level as is possible.AA
Figure 7.21. Adjusting leveling screws,
(2) Yari kata -batter boards.
A batler board is a temporary marker indicating a horizontal plane, as well as the center of a
wall or post, obtained from a benchmark (see F'tgure 7.22),
The suigui (stakes) are11/a in. square and about 36 in. long. They are staked around the
building location, off-set from the actual building position (see Figure 7'23). The tops of the
suigul are formed in one ol the shapes shown in Figure7.24: (a)yahazu or (b)isuka. These
shapes are used to discourage anything being placed on top of a stake, and to make it
easier to spot if the stakes have been pounded after they have been set.
fhe mizunuki(ties) are boards about s/a in. thick, 31/z in. wide, and 48 in. long, and are lree
of warps or twists. Usually Japanese cedar is used. The tops of the ties are planed.
There are two types ol yarikata (batter board)l llie sumi yarikata (corner board), and the
hira yarik;ata (straight board). To construct a corner board, three stakes and two tie boards
are required. There are two types of straight board, One is to indicate the location of the
center line ol a wall center, the other is to support tho taut line and to keep it from sagging
when spanning a long distance. To construct a straight board, two stakes and one ti6 board
are required.

Next, using the bench marks, the center lines ol posts and walls are calculated and are

Determlning the helght ol a king post
(i) Basic concept, The position of rool f raming members is determined by the height of the
king post.
ln Figure 7.27, ahorizontal line passes through the pivot point' which is an intersection of
the vertical cenler line ot a post and the bottom surtace line of a rafter. ll you draw a line

## S. 90

f 66 Framlng and Securlng
from pivot point to pivot point across a room or building, it is called a "base line." The
dimension a is the distance from the pivot point to the location of the king post, and b
equals a x roof slope. (C equals the length of a ratler segmont above length a, or c=17+F.
Once we know the slope of the roof and distance a, the hoight of the king post can be determined.
Example: ln a case were a = 36 inches and the roof slope equals 5/10 (using Figure
7.28) then b = 36 x 5/10 = 18 and c = V36tTl€t= 40.25 inches,
Figure 7.27. Determining the height ol a king post.
BASE LINE (FUN)
t
Figure 7.28, The basic triangle,
Framldg and Securing 16Z
Kyoro framing: The top surface of the spanning bJam in kyoro framing is usuaily higher
than the base line. since the king post sits on the spanning beam, the length of the king post
at point A is determined by deducting the disiance between the base line and the top surface
of the spanning beam and lhe height ot the purlin from length b, which was determined be_
fore, An eiample of this is given in Figure 7,29.
t'
I
Figure 7.29. Kyoro traming
Figure 7.30 Orioki traming,
orioki framing,'The top surtace of the spanning beam in orioki lraming is generally lower
than the base line. To determine the length of the king post, the distance lrom the top sur
face of the beam to the base line must be added to length b in Figure 7.30 since the king
posts rest on the top surtace of the beam, Next, the depth of the purlin*the distance from
the king post pivot to the bottom of the purlin-has to be deducted from length b. since the
purlin is a common member in either kind of framing, ils depth must be deducted from length
b in both cases.
where the spanning beam or purlin is a log, the method for determining the lengths of rhe
king posts is identical to the one explained above. However, the depth of the log at point A
will not be a constant. For this reason each depth is noted on a board drawing, as part of a
grid system, and the depth at point A is determined accordingly.

length or run ts in units of either 1 or 10. The rise is the vertical distance and is the dimension
which can be adjusted to obtain the desired slope, The greater the rise, the greater will be

Tatekata (construction) soquence:
(1) Placing lhe dodai (mudsill): After cleaning thq top surface of the foundation wall, the exterior mudsill face location, obtained from the batter boards, is marked. lf the center line
were marked, it would be covered when the mudsill was set in place and would therefore
serve no purpos€, lnstead, the line of the mudsill's outside face is marked and the mudsill
aligned to it.
Next, the locations ol the anchor bolts are marked and holes are drilled for them. Then
the mudsill is secured to the {oundation.
(2)Erection:fhe scaflolding is constructed set back about24 jn. trom the exterior walls.
Placing the posts: The posts are placed according to the marking sequence. The
ashigatame beams (bottom plates), sashigamoi beams (headers), and others which are
connected to the posts with mortise and tenon joints are then assembled on the ground and
raised to set the posts in place. In lhe case ot a single story residence, the noki gefa (eave
beams) and the lsuma'barl (full width) beams are inserted into the posts' tenons, and, using
long members such as lies for temporary diagonal braces, the house is constructed starting
from one corner.
ln the case of a two story residence, the do sashl (equivalent to the top plates) are
secured to the toshi bashira (continuous posts), and at the same time inserted into the top
tenon of the kuda bashira (single story floor posts), Then the'temporary diagonal brace is
put in place. For the installation of the second floor obari and kobari (beams), the same procedure is followed as in single floor construction. After the axial membors are framed,
tateokoshi (plumbing) follows,
(3) rateokoshi-plumbing posts; This is a method for plumbing posts using asage furi (a
type of guide), rope, turnbuckle, jacks, and olher pieces (see Figure 7.32).

The sage lurl is a guide tool made at the site from readily available materials such as

tool is placed along a post and a prumb bob is dangred from point A. when point B is

To adjust members so that they are square one another, prace a rope around them as
shown in Figure 7,34, and with a stick at the middle twist the tope to pull the members towards each other.
During lateokoshi (prumbing) notch out members for the placement of the diagonar
braces. secure these braces with large nails or pin anchors. when the/uku guml (wallfram_
ing) is completed the roof lraming can begin. Framing horizontal members such as the
nakabiki bari and chimune beams in sequence, and setting the koya bari beamat the end,
roof members such as rhe koya lsuka (end posts), and nru bari (headers) are put in place.
Framing is completed when the ridge beam js installed,
The assembling ol members lor western roof framing (the truss system) is done on the
ground; bolts and pin anchors are used to secure the members.
To raise the truss and large beams, a single crane, called a bozu, is constructed at the
site. The bozu is made of a tall log erected vertically and secured with four ropes tied to the
top, with their other ends attached to pegs in the ground. A pulley is placed on the top ol the
pote ano a rope is passed through it, Heavy members are then tied to this rope, raised
gently, and secured to the attaching members one al a ilme. ln doing so, the proper ptacement of pins, anchors, wedges,;borts, and other connectors must not be forgotten.

other. There are variQus right angle jolnls such as mortise and tenon, lap, dovetail' spllne'

lntuture,shiguchishouldbemadesimpler,relyingonmelalconneclorsasmuchas
oolr,o,u. lt'is nJcessary to invesilgate into a reinforcing member which will not cause the
wood to chip or to leave cracks as wood expands and contracts'

conditions:

3
Various Types of Fasteners
Traditionally, various fasleners such as keys, wedges, and pins have been used to secure
and tie down pieces at their end and righl angle joints, Their size and nomenclature differs
according to their use, These fasteners are made from hard wood. when they are used, the
hole or slot which is lo accept them must be constructed so lhat the fasteners, when being
installed, will pull the pieces together tightly.
Today, metal fasleners have in certain situations replaced the traditional wooden ones.
These reinforcing fasteners, if used properly, can simplify the design of end loints and right
angle joinls, and can also increase their efficiency.

Jiku Gumi-Framing
After completlon of the foundation work, parts of the architectural skeleton such as mud.
sills, posts, beams, headers, etc., are put together. This ls called framing (iiku gumf).
ln order to conslruct the necessary joint cut-outs on the framing members, drawings
(eizu ita, kosakuzu ot kanbanzu),* rulers, and if necessary, kanabakari (detailed lraming
sectional drawings) are used.
There are two points which need lo be carefully considered for actual construclion work.
One is to keep the joint cut"outs tg a mlnimum so that a momber's structural strength ls not
groatly reduced. The other is to keep lhe ioint details as easy as possible lo construct,
It is important that the framing will be able to resist various external forces and can
maintain its shape without change. To achieve this, use diagonal braces and angle braces
in equal quantities whenever possible. Also, lorm as many triangular conneclions for both
horlzontal and vertical framing as possible, and utilize bolts and other melal connectors to
tighten connections.
* Note: Eizu ita, kosakuzu or kanbanzu are not architect'$ drawings. These are drawings
made on wooden boards by the master carpenter showing the traming sequence in which
to frame t[e skeletal structural members. The drawing, of course, does not alter the
architoct's design.

Japanese Style Roof Framing
Japanese style roor f raming is composed simpry of pracing the end post (rsuka) verticaly on
top of the beam, and connecring end posts at opposite ends horizonraily with purlins and
ridges (mune) on which rarters (targth) are praced perpendicurarry. since the beams of
Japanese style roof framing are simpre beams which support ail the concentrated roads
from the end posts, they must be be strong enough to resist the bending moment.
The Japanese styre or roof f raming, due to its ease and simpricity of construction is used
malnly on small buildings where beam spacing is short. Lumber becomes uneconomical
and unstabro as the spacing of beams becomes rarger. rn cases where the spacing of
beams is large, western style framing is used.
Beams' whether they are made of rogs, curved rumber or regurar rumber, can easiry be
framed' However, it is important to set the rumber in such a way that when warpage occurs,
the top face or the beam curv€s up or erse the beam wiir have a negative bend,
Beams used in the yoiir1 koya f raming method and nobori beams which are piaced at a
stoep slope, are usuaily praced in such a way that when warpage occurs, the top surface
will curve down, 4nd beams wiil have a positive bend. This is done because of fear that the
force will push oul the noki baribeam ralher than fear of it not being able to resist the loads.

Melhod for dotermlnlng the length ol the hlp Jack rafter,
This melhod using gancri gane (a special carpenter's square) counls each spaclng unit determlned by lh6
carpenler's square, roflectlng the rise and run of the slope as shown in the illuslration. lf the unit space ends in
a partial unit, such as 7cm or 5cm, thig panial unit is to be consldered the run ot the slopo and the rise is then
datermined by a proportional method or wlth carpenter's square. The illustration below is an example uslng a
slope of fo or 5 rlse tor every 10 of run.
The ligure (right) illustrates a method using gangl gana 1o
measure lhe lsngth ol lhe ratters, This melhod
accuralely measures the length of the rafters and hlp
ralter.
FFONT FACF OF H/FOKOMAI FACIA
To enlarge the unit spacing, lhe run ls sel al 30 cm.
(runxS)*30cm.
(risex3)='lscm.

to
Western Style Roof Framing
Slnce w€stern rooJs ar€ f ramed with beams, ratters, end studs, braces, and other members
constructod lnto truss€s, or in other words, as a single unlt, they can be categorized lnto
two malor types, the shinzuka truss with a vertical center web and the tal zuku truss without
a vertical center web.
. Since westorn roof framing is completely dilferent from Japanese roof framing in
resistance to lorces and loads, the strength of each member of the truss must be
consldered. The roof framing as a unit is constructed quite logically, but the braces to resist
torque, diagonal bracings, and spaced beams to resist wind and external forces, and other
structural members, each have to be f ramed to form a monolithlc structure. The supporting
members such as webs, and tle pleces such as bolts are shaped and construoted after the
actual dimenslons requlred have been determined.

To gel approximate English dimensions, divide millimeters by 3 and multiply lhe answer by %
For example 1ztnm/t = 4 x % = t/r".

Gable Construction
Motal rooflng.
The construction method lor metal roofing is discussed below
1. The materials used lor metal roofing are tin, alumlnum, stainless steel, Monel, zlnc, copper, and others
2. There are wood traming sy6tems with and wilhout rafters.
a. Framing systems using rafters: Ratters are placed with the same spacing as the ribs lor the seams on metal
rooting. When this is done the ribs are preventod from sliding because they are nalled onto the rafters. The
sneathing is most olt6n nalled on but when don6 wilh care it ls grooved in. Since not all the nails for the ribs
can be expected not lo hit the ralters, the sheathing used should have a minimum thicknoss ol 12mm. Wood
planks are not the only materials used for sheathing. There are insulation board, plywood, partlole board, and
cement board. Wh€re lhe sheathhg is of planks, insulation boards are Inslalled for thermal protoction. On top
of these is placed the waterproofing membrane, such as bullding papor and felts.
b. Framing sysloms without rafters: ln this rnethod the sheathlng is secured directly to the moya beam The
spacing oilne fioya beams should be about 60mm, less than in lhe situation wh€ro rafters are used The
ihickness of the sheathing should be at least 18mm-20mm and should be tongue and grooved, The nails used
should be long. The hanagaraml usod musl be greater than 40 x 20mm, or else the top and the bollom ol lhe
6av6 will not align.
Varletloo and appllcatlons ol mstal roollng.

TH€ LENGTH MUST Be LESS THAN 4smm
(PARTIAL SHEETS SHOULD B€ AVOIDED )

Taruki kei lacla has a deplh lwice thal of lhe common rafter, and a width

APPENDIX I
Monlaku-Forlune Scale
Monjaku is a scale which divldes 9 sun 6 /u* into I equal parts, each named as listed below
lo determine the good or bad omen lor a building of a given dimension The dimension of a
space is obtained with a regular scale which is then placed next to the moniaku scale on
which the point to be transferred is read. Though not many modern scales include this
scale, the names are given so they can be referred to in case the topic is dilscussed at the
construction site. 
L
Zalr HapPiness and wealth.
Byo: A great amount of sickness in the tamily and the continuation ol mislortune.
Rlr Bad relationships within the llmily and tamily breakup.
Gi: All wishes will come true, and there will be happiness,
Kan: Much happiness and success achieved, except as a merchant 
l
Ko: Many descendents and much happiness, However, the home will be
burglarized and be unluckY.
Gal: Much mlsfortune and death. The worst fortune.
Klchir Great fortune in everything with manS'ldescendents and a good family name'
Example: The widlh oJ a gate is 5 shaku (about 5 teet). Apcording to the rnonlaku scale, this
falls on gl. As explained above, this is a good omen, arid as a dimension, the width of the
gate is very good.
There was also a scale which gavo the circum{erence of a circle after obtaining the circle's diameter. The diameter is read on the front edge of the scale-for example, 1 sun, The
circumference scale is on the opposite edge. By transferring it reads 3.141 thus the circumference is 3.14 sun. (See example shown in Appendix Figure 1.)

about /e inch,

APPENDIX 2
The Okane - Large Carpenter's Square
(a) Before beginning the loundation work in the construction of a building, the leveling and
setting up of the batter boards have to be done. For these jobs, the squareness and levelness of a structure are obtained using either the levels or lhe okane. Establishing squareness with lne okane is called "314:5 no hi", ot "43 wo furu" ("shake the 43"), This method
utilizes the geometric relationships of a triangle with legs 3:4:5,
Mark center line
/ wllh sumitsubo.
Appendix Figure 2. Okane (largecarpenter's square)
Method for making the okane.
(i) Tools and materials: The materials needed are Japanese cedar boards: one board 1 l/a in,
thick, 4 in. wide, and 10 ft. long, and two boards 6 ft. long; and five to ten nails, 1/z in, long,
A hammer, plane, carpenter's square, measuring tape, chalk line, and marker ar€ the tools
needed,
(ii) Qonstruction sequence: Begin by planing the boards. The edges (sides) ot the boards
should be planed straight. Next, mark a center line on each board with the chalk line as
shown in Appendix Figure 2. Place the boards on flat ground, the 6 ft. boards placed perpendicular to each other, and the 10 ft. board to be placed diagonally, forming a triangle.
The distances between the intersecting points of the center lines should be in a ratio of
3:4:5, as shown. When the boards are arranged in this ratio, secure them with at least 4
nails at each point.
APPENDIX 3
Method for Maklng a Ksnzao (Measurlng) Scale
Kenzao, or shakuzue by its other name, is a scale for locating the position ol a partition's
center line, and of footings, beams, and other framing members. The scales, one for
horizontal dimensions, the othpr for vertical dimensions, are made to simplily the work at
the site.
The materials usually used are Japanese cedar boards with very lew defects. The pieces
should be planed on all four sides, moasuring 1t/q in,xlly'a in, and about 10 ft, to 13 ft, long"
On one side of the boards, marks are made 20 in. apart. The scales for horizontal and
vertical measures are placed side by side so the marks on both scales are made at th€
same time in one motion. lf the marks were made separately, due to the variation in thickness of the marked lines, differences would occur. Thus it is very important that the boards
be placed side by side and marked simultaneously, Furthermore, never make two vertical or
two horizontal scales. Always make one and always use that specific one, At the beginning
mark, point "o," a protrusion should be made to prevent damage, as well as to act as an indicator. This projection is called lshi tsukl. (See Appendix Figure 3.)

APPENDIX 4
Ceremonies
(1) Architectural ceremony recognizes that building one's own house is one of the major
events of one's lile. To some, such an event is impossible, but in this modorn age, due to
the various loans available, building a house has become a reality for many people. Nevertheless, it is an experience which cannot easily be repeated.
since building a house is such an important event, it is natural to want everything lo go
well' so a ceremony is held at the start of construction. Over the ages various Shinto and
Buddhist ceremonies have been performed, Here, only the mosl common ceremonies are
described.
(2) The ceremonies.
(i) Jichinsai: ll is said the origin of this ceremony was intended as a memorial for the numerous plants' various insects, and small creatures that would be destroyed during building on
the prope(y. lt also asked the many gods of the property tor their consent to the construction. usually this was a shinto ceremony, but there are also some similar Buddhist
ceremonies.
This ceremony is held on a day especia|y selected for its auspiciousness.
The lollowing information musl be given to the priest for conducting tne jichinsaii
.Name of the ceremony.
.Name of the constructjon (e.g., residence).
.Name of the owner.
.Name of the supervisor or conlractor.
This inlormation will be read into the rites. Appendix Figure 6 shows
people at the site during the ceremony,
(1) objects used in the ceremony: stands, a basket, plates, and the priest's tools tor the
ritual and lhe sacred tree.
the position of the

(2) Purified goodsi rice, wine, sea salt, rice cake, spring water, and products ol the sea,
mountain, and fields, totaling either 5 or 7 (an odd number is preierable). Examples of
mountain products: fruits, mushrooms. Fiold products: vegetables (but not onions). Sea
products: lish, dried foods, shellfish, (The fish's head must be on lts left side, with its belly
facing the altar.)
The anangement of these things will vary depending on the priest, so prior discussion
with the priest will be necessary. ll ritual digging is to be done, the shovels must be brand
new, and their handles wrapped with while paper tied with sacred paper string.
(3) Sequence: The ceremony is canied out in the following sequencei opening ceremony,
shubatsu;descent of god, koshln-no-gl. Reading ol the following rites: kensen, noritosaio,
setsuma-sanma, tamagushi holen, fessen, ascent of god, and closing.
Appendix 4 375
(ii)Jotoshlklj The ceremony oliotoshiki, widely known as lalamae, is performed for every
building project. lt is done where construction has reached the point when the ridge beam is
about to be installed. With steel frame construction, it is performed when the last beam is
about to be installed. Jotoshiki is intended 1o thank the gods for their protection, for the architectural construction, and for ensuring that the construction has progressed smoothly to
the present stage, as well as to ask them for continuing lavot until the completion of the
work.
The ceremonial procedures are similar to those ol iichinsai,
(1) Ihe munaluda * ridge notice board. Since ancient times, the rnunafuda has been used
to record the structure's particulars, lt is tied to the south face of the muna tsuka (end post
supporting the ridge beam) with hemp string, A Japanese cypress board is used, 6 ft long,
12 in. wide at the top, 107e in. wide at the bottom, andllq in. thick. Today, a smaller version
with the same proportions is used. (See Appendix Figure 7.)
Appendix Figure 7. Munafuda (ridge notice board)'
(2) The helgushi heizuka, hei-no-kushi, or heishin is called sanpo (three sticks) because
three heikushi are used. Usually it is made of Japanese cypress board measuring 1/zin'xlz
in.x12 ft. (1 jo 2sun). There are Smaller ones with dimensions of 3/e in. square and 9t/zin. or
35 7z in. long.
